  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      });

<span id="freeTextContainer15673367385179674911">Desde su aparición en 1953, este libro de relatos del mexicano Juan Rulfo se ha traducido a más de veinticinco lenguas y ha dado lugar a múltiples y permanentes reediciones en los países de lengua hispana. Esta edición, única revisada y autorizada por la Fundación Juan Rulfo, debe ser considerada como la definitiva.</span>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1587783-any-thoughts-about-which-translation-into">Any thoughts about which translation into English is the better one? It looks like the Stavans (The plain in flames) is more recent than the Schade (The burning plain...). Thanks.</a>
</div>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/27697731-peera-songk-nnatham"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/27697731-peera-songk-nnatham">Peera Songkünnatham</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I've read the three English-language versions. I think the new British version is the best, as it successfully strikes a middle ground between the sim<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I've read the three English-language versions. I think the new British version is the best, as it successfully strikes a middle ground between the simplified, idiomatic Schade, and the jarringly poetic Stavans. Beechinor succeeds in capturing the sensorial complexity of Rulfo's stories without watering down their oral quality like Schade.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/751432325" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/751432325?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780292701328" href="/book/show/38788.The_Burning_Plain_and_Other_Stories">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer751432325" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14231058829231098691">An outstanding collection of snippets, ever-literary and uniquely blissfully Mexican. These stories are timeless. And it is precisely that quality, that of timelessness, which best describes the setting which pervades throughout these incendiary vignettes. The best are short enough to be parables--"We're Very Poor" (heart-wrenching), "The burning plain" (epic--aka where Cormack McCarthy got his prose) & "Anacleto Morones" (the anti "8 1/2") were my favorites. The leitmotifs are beautiful and mea</span>
  <span id="freeText14231058829231098691" style="display:none">An outstanding collection of snippets, ever-literary and uniquely blissfully Mexican. These stories are timeless. And it is precisely that quality, that of timelessness, which best describes the setting which pervades throughout these incendiary vignettes. The best are short enough to be parables--"We're Very Poor" (heart-wrenching), "The burning plain" (epic--aka where Cormack McCarthy got his prose) &amp; "Anacleto Morones" (the anti "8 1/2") were my favorites. The leitmotifs are beautiful and meaningful--they are exceptional in many respects. Like strong, strenuous gulps of mescal from different times &amp; areas, each one represents a reality that is harrowing &amp; pinpoint specific to Mexico-- "el llano en llamas"...it still burns!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14231058829231098691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_168753881" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168753881" />
    <a title="Mike Puma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4193758-mike-puma"><img alt="Mike Puma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434667514p2/4193758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/168753881?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Puma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Puma" href="/user/show/4193758-mike-puma">Mike Puma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780292701328" href="/book/show/38788.The_Burning_Plain_and_Other_Stories">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mike by: </span>
      Roberto Bolaño
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4193758-mike-puma?shelf=mexican-author">mexican-author</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer168753881" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17234493742274796839"><p>Staggering, bleak page-turners that leave you wanting more—more stories, more Rulfo. Each story is complete—nothing left wanting and nothing extraneous.</p><p>Imagine stories told by a character from one of Cormac McCarthy’s southwestern novels. Imagine that character retelling a story by Flannery O’Connor (e.g., A Good Man Is Hard to Find). [I almost…almost…suggested: imagine a dictionary-less Cormac McCarthy doing Flannery O’Connor, but I knew what the perverse among you would make of that]. It doesn</p></span>
  <span id="freeText17234493742274796839" style="display:none"><p>Staggering, bleak page-turners that leave you wanting more—more stories, more Rulfo. Each story is complete—nothing left wanting and nothing extraneous.</p><p>Imagine stories told by a character from one of Cormac McCarthy’s southwestern novels. Imagine that character retelling a story by Flannery O’Connor (e.g., A Good Man Is Hard to Find). [I almost…almost…suggested: imagine a dictionary-less Cormac McCarthy doing Flannery O’Connor, but I knew what the perverse among you would make of that]. It doesn’t require much of a creative leap to imagine John Grady Cole or Billy Parham (the Border trilogy) or the kid (Blood Meridian) hearing these stories of gloom, ominously, on any of their excursions into Mexico. “No Dogs Bark”—the negative, reversed image of <i>The Road</i>.</p><p>I’m not one ordinarily attracted to short stories, but I’m so impressed by this collection that I’m even more anxious to pick up the compilations of Javier Marías—I suspect he won’t suffer, given my predisposition, but DAMN, Rulfo’s concision seems a direct challenge to my expectations for anything written by JM. </p><p>Bravo, Señor Rulfo! Gracias, Señor Bolaño, for bringing him to my attention. It sucks that the thanks and praise must be offered to the dead.</p><p>I almost can’t believe it—one friend and one reviewer I follow have rated this book—something’s wrong here, something’s terribly wrong!</p><br /><br /></span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12950567583589721945"><blockquote>
  <i>“You’ll be seeing that wind that blows over Luvina. It’s dark. They say because it’s full of volcano sand; anyway, it’s a black air. You’ll see it. It takes hold of things in Luvina as if it was going to bite them. And there are lots of days when it takes the roofs off the houses as if they were hats, leaving the bare walls uncovered. Then it scratches like it had nails: you hear it morning and night, hour after hour without stopping, scraping the walls, tearing off strips of earth, digging w</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12950567583589721945" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“You’ll be seeing that wind that blows over Luvina. It’s dark. They say because it’s full of volcano sand; anyway, it’s a black air. You’ll see it. It takes hold of things in Luvina as if it was going to bite them. And there are lots of days when it takes the roofs off the houses as if they were hats, leaving the bare walls uncovered. Then it scratches like it had nails: you hear it morning and night, hour after hour without stopping, scraping the walls, tearing off strips of earth, digging with its sharp shovel under the doors, until you feel it boiling inside of you as if it was going to remove the hinges of your very bones. You’ll see.”</i>
</blockquote><br />In these fifteen bleak, savage short stories, “impotence and despair reign, and death rattles in the scorching air, the howling wind, the throttling dust of the plain.” They’re presumably even more powerful in the original Spanish. Though I can hardly see how it would be possible to improve them.</span>

<div id="review_1081965006" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1081965006" />
    <a title="Stephen P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p"><img alt="Stephen P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461732018p2/11602286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1081965006?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen P" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p">Stephen P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780292701328" href="/book/show/38788.The_Burning_Plain_and_Other_Stories">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those open to the many facets of the human heart.</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Stephen by: </span>
      Ben Winch
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11602286-stephen-p?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1081965006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11225952604697623542"><br/> Holding the spine of the book in the palm of my hand, having read Rulfo's great novel Pedro Páramo , I felt the sear of my skin before opening the front cover. This short story collection's primal bleakness, savagery, rumbled within. Hazemat gear in toe I pried open the book and stood back. My hand burnt, cracked the pain oozing upward, outward, I stared at the sparse simple prose. A style any writer might be proud of to cover many examples of a staid story line. An aesthetic which yearns to be</span>
  <span id="freeText11225952604697623542" style="display:none"><br /> Holding the spine of the book in the palm of my hand, having read Rulfo's great novel Pedro Páramo , I felt the sear of my skin before opening the front cover. This short story collection's primal bleakness, savagery, rumbled within. Hazemat gear in toe I pried open the book and stood back. My hand burnt, cracked the pain oozing upward, outward, I stared at the sparse simple prose. A style any writer might be proud of to cover many examples of a staid story line. An aesthetic which yearns to be read out loud. Indeed Rulfo's voice speaks in one's ear, remains unruffled in one's mind.<br /><br />Bandaging my hand-my wife says there is nothing there. I beg to differ with her. That I didn't take first aid forty years ago for nothing. Asking for some room I pointed out this was first-responder work.<br /><br />It is. It is Reader First Responder Work. There is no training even though I thought I was well trained. These stories instantaneously jump into the bleakest of environments. The sun is either blazing down with such heat as to rip the stamina of a persons soul or so cold as to freeze internal life in favor of the necessity of survival with little time for endurance to dream of hope. Rulfo has bared the land where almost nothing can grow. Decimated, sheared, it has not been placed to support and propagate human life. So, why not leave. Move elsewhere. Go North where money is to be made by crossing the border into the United States and returning as others have? Return? Yes. You see this is where their dead are. One cannot leave one's dead. The living maybe.<br /><br />This is a collection of leavings. More important it is a collection of being hunted. From story to story characters are hunted by the unremitting sun, the soil that is no more, one more mountain of jagged rock to be scaled, the last bits of food to be eaten, false religions, government armies, soldiers of rebellion, outlaws, the steaming blood of revenge no matter how many years passed. Time seeped by long enough where the people appear indifferent. Cold blooded, cold calculated. Rulfo with the slenderest of brush strokes crosses these people, we readers, over the line from indifference to acceptance. This is merely the way life is and is expected to be. Laying out on the arid plains he hones us in on a look at the primal savagery of the human heart. A broodingly dark place to live within while reading though Rulfo's aesthetic of the writing of the plains-works well as a geographic location and writing style-is so skilled it underlines, setting fire, to the brutality while providing a sumptuous feast to those who appreciate the temperance of great literature.<br /><br />Where has he been? He wrote very little. Raising a family and working common jobs he wrote when he could at night producing I believe another collection of short stories and a short novel which all readers have a responsibility to themselves to explore,Pedro Páramo.<br /><br />This collection is not a series of linked short works building up to a common theme. It is the same theme from the beginning explored in a number of fascinating examples with fascinating characters. Rulfo's ability to focus on details opens worlds in short spaces. The only fault I found but resulting in the loss of a star in the dust of the plains is that, though still enjoyable, a few stories were repetitious. This slim volume is but 147 pages. I think it is possible the editor-publisher needed to fill it out so that…? It would look filled with more presents and therefore more marketable?<br /><br />This was a great read in understanding these people, myself, others, the static beat of the human heart and the ways surroundings carve the niche.<br /><br />I give much thanks to Ben Winch for introducing me to Rulfo. I doubt I would have found him otherwise and life would have been that much less.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11225952604697623542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3158127729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3158127729" />
    <a title="Paul Fulcher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3250759-paul-fulcher"><img alt="Paul Fulcher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542271295p2/3250759.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3158127729?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Fulcher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Fulcher" href="/user/show/3250759-paul-fulcher">Paul Fulcher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780995632011" href="/book/show/52727606-el-llano-in-flames">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3250759-paul-fulcher?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3250759-paul-fulcher?shelf=republic-of-consciousness-2020">republic-of-consciousness-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3158127729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12584195291274926247">Longlisted for the 2020 Republic of Consciouness Prize<br/> <br/>Pedro Páramo by Juan Rulfo, published in 1955, is hailed by many, most notably Gabriel Garcia Marquez, as a key development in Latin American, and indeed world, literature.<br/> <br/>However, I had not read his equally important short story collection El Llano en Llamas, published in 1955, so it was a delight to see it longlisted for my favourite literary prize.<br/> <br/>This is actually the third translation to English, although the first published outside of</span>
  <span id="freeText12584195291274926247" style="display:none">Longlisted for the 2020 Republic of Consciouness Prize<br /> <br />Pedro Páramo by Juan Rulfo, published in 1955, is hailed by many, most notably Gabriel Garcia Marquez, as a key development in Latin American, and indeed world, literature.<br /> <br />However, I had not read his equally important short story collection El Llano en Llamas, published in 1955, so it was a delight to see it longlisted for my favourite literary prize.<br /> <br />This is actually the third translation to English, although the first published outside of the US:<br /> <br />- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38788.The_Burning_Plain_and_Other_Stories" title="The Burning Plain and Other Stories by Juan Rulfo" rel="nofollow noopener">The Burning Plain and Other Stories</a> by George D. Schade (1967)<br /><br />- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13699329.The_Plain_in_Flames" title="The Plain in Flames by Juan Rulfo" rel="nofollow noopener">The Plain in Flames</a> by Ilan Stavans with Harold Augenbram (2012), which added two stories that the author himself added in 1970, "La herencia de Matilde Arcángel" (as “The Legacy of Matilde Arcángel”) and El día del derrumbe (as “The Day of the Collapse”)<br /><br />- and this by Stephen Beechinor (2019), which also includes all 17 stories.<br /> <br />Beechinor explains how he came to the book and why he re-translated it in this fascinating interview: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://structomagazine.co.uk/stephen-beechinor-talks-el-llano-in-flames/">https://structomagazine.co.uk/stephen...</a>.   On the choice of title for the collection he explains:  <blockquote>El Llano or El Llano Grande is the name of the arid, treeless, shrubless, birdless flatland in Jalisco, Mexico, where the stories are set. It’s shown in the relief map on the cover of this translation.<br /> <br />In El Llano we have a proper noun, a toponym, a place name that describes the land. And as a general noun, a llano is a dry plain of sometimes great extension, a feature of the northern parts of south America and the south-western US. Like veldt or steppe or glen or bayou, a llano is a geographical particularity and to transpose the particular into the generic would be to traduce it. And finally, typographically, that initial ‘Ll’ digraph holds your eye nicely; it snags in the mind in just the right way.</blockquote>Ivan Stavans' perspective is at these interviews: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://biblioklept.org/2013/05/16/you-have-all-of-a-sudden-seen-the-color-of-existence-under-a-new-light-a-conversation-with-ilan-stavans-part-1/">https://biblioklept.org/2013/05/16/yo...</a> and <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://biblioklept.org/2014/01/22/latin-americans-have-become-contemporaries-with-the-rest-of-the-world-a-conversation-with-ilan-stavans-part-2/">https://biblioklept.org/2014/01/22/la...</a>.   And his choice of title:<blockquote>The title in Spanish has the alliteration – El Llano en Llamas. Llano. Llamas. In English, the first translation was The Burning Plain, which is so dull, so plain, so uninteresting. I immediately said I’ll do it, but it has to be The Plain in Flames, which plays with the alliteration. The Juan Rulfo Foundation said “we love it.” The publisher said “we can’t do it” – because people have already connected The Burning Plain with Rulfo, and if you change the title, you can lose readers. And I said I’m not doing that. If we don’t have “The Plain in Flames,” I won’t do it. And finally we were able to convince them. So they resisted for marketing reasons. That’s something that translators often have to deal with.</blockquote>This collection is perhaps less formally inventive than the hallucinatory Pedro Páramo, but is important for giving direct voice to the inhabitants of the llano, its acute psychological realism and the brilliant economy of the writing.  The opening story here, 'They gave us The land' / 'Nos han dado la tierra' is one of a number that focus on the disappointing reality of life in post-revolutionary Mexico.  The narrator is one of a dwindling group of men travel over the arid llano to the barren territory they've been assigned as part of land reform.  As they struggle in the heat, a dark cloud passes overhead, giving them hope, but only a solitary raindrop falls.<br /> <br /><i>The earth sucks at the drop that fell by mistake and swallows it down<br /> <br />Who in hell would make this llano so vast? To serve what purpose, huh?<br />...<br />Still as a boy I never saw it rain on the llano, not ever, not in the proper sense of rain.<br /> <br />No, the llano isn’t good for much.  Not a rabbit, not a bird to be seen.  Not a thing.  Other than a few mangy huisache shrubs and the odd scrap of grass and the blades all curled up; other than that, not a thing.  <br />...<br />Such land and so much of it, and what for?</i><br /> <br />For translation comparison: Stevens version<br /><i>And the drop that fell by mistake is devoured by the earth, which makes it disappear into its thirst.<br /> <br />Who the hell would make this plain so big? What’s it good for, eh?<br />...<br />All in all, I know that from the time I was a boy, I have never seen rain fall on the plain, what you might call rain.<br /> <br />No, the plain isn’t good for anything. There are neither rabbits nor birds. There is nothing. Except for a few huizache trees and one or two spots of zacate with their leaves curled up; aside from that, there’s nothing.<br />...<br />Such vast land for nothing.</i><br /> <br />The original:<br /><i>Y a la gota caída por equivocación se la come la tierra y la desaparece en su sed.<br />...<br />¿Quién diablos haría este llano tan grande? ¿Para qué sirve, eh?<br />...<br />Con todo, yo sé que desde que yo era muchacho, no vi llover nunca sobre el llano, lo que se llama llover.<br /> <br />No, el llano no es cosa que sirva. No hay ni conejos ni pájaros. No hay nada. A no ser unos cuantos huizaches trespeleques y una que otra manchita de zacate con las hojas enroscadas; a no ser eso, no hay nada.<br />...<br />Tanta y tamaña tierra para nada.</i><br /> <br />'The man' / 'El hombre' is striking in form.  The narrative style has some similarities to the also-RoC longlisted Love as it switches back and forth between two perspectives, here the man of the title, who is fleeing after performing a revenge killing, and his pursuer, the man he incorrectly believes he killed, now seeking revenge in turn, a scenario the reader has to gradually piece together.  A final section is narrated in the form of one half of an interview with the authorities by a shepherd boy who witnessed the denoument of the tale as the man finds himself trapped by the river, just as the two men are trapped in a cycle of revenge.   Rulfo had originally titled the story 'Where the river runs in circles'.<br /> <br />The title story narrates a snapshot of the rather chaotic and un-heroic story of the revolution itself.  <br />Other favourites included:<br /><br />'Remember' which perhaps best captures the nature of oral reminiscence (it reminded me of afternoons with my father and uncles who would refer to people as “him what used to go with her from..”)<br /><br /><br />And my favourite of all, Luvina, the story that paves the way towards Pedro Páramo. Another translation comparison:<br /><br />Here:<br /><i>People in Luvina say dreams well up from those ravines, but the wind’s about all I ever know to rise out of there, clamouring like it was being piped up through reeds from way below. A wind that snuffs our even the woody nightshade - those glum, bitty plants that just about thrive in a scrape of dirt; clinging on for all their worth to upland cliffs.</i>   <br /> <br />Stavans:<br /><i>People in Luvina say dreams rise out of those ravines; but the only thing I ever saw rise up from there was the wind, whirling, as if it had been imprisoned down below in reed pipes. A wind that doesn't even let bittersweet grow: those sad little plants can barely live, holding on for all they're worth to the side of the cliffs in these hills, as if they were smeared onto the earth.</i><br /><br />Original: <br /><i>Dicen los de Luvina que de aquellas barrancas suben los sueños; pero yo lo único que vi subir fue el viento, en tremolina, como si allá abajo lo hubieran encañonado en tubos de carrizo. Un viento que no deja crecer ni a las dulcamaras: esas plantitas tristes que apenas si pueden vivir un poco untadas en la tierra, agarradas con todas sus manos al despeñadero de los montes. </i><br /> <br />3.5 stars - worthy but I am not 100% convinced a new translation was needed, and I feel Pedro Paramo remains the more important of the author's work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12584195291274926247" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780292701328" href="/book/show/38788.The_Burning_Plain_and_Other_Stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer262791508" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12455117175973971454">F**k me this is good! As good as <i>Pedro Paramo</i>! Rulfo is a master, the equal of anyone anywhere anytime. These stories are so elemental they are as if hewn from stone. The opener, 'Macario', is a mini-miracle - a monologue by an idiot child so convincing we forget it is fiction, forget we are reading almost. And so it is with every story here. They don't 'jump off the page' like so many supposedly virtuosic feats of literary ventriloquism; they insinuate themselves quietly, seem to reach us intra</span>
  <span id="freeText12455117175973971454" style="display:none">F**k me this is good! As good as <i>Pedro Paramo</i>! Rulfo is a master, the equal of anyone anywhere anytime. These stories are so elemental they are as if hewn from stone. The opener, 'Macario', is a mini-miracle - a monologue by an idiot child so convincing we forget it is fiction, forget we are reading almost. And so it is with every story here. They don't 'jump off the page' like so many supposedly virtuosic feats of literary ventriloquism; they insinuate themselves quietly, seem to reach us intravenously. Subtlety and humility, the near-utter subjugation of the writer to his text, a sense of place so vivid it consumes. Hail Juan Rulfo! The star that shines brightest burns fastest.<br /></span>

<div id="review_3158075789" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3158075789" />
    <a title="Gumble&#39;s Yard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35482263-gumble-s-yard"><img alt="Gumble&#39;s Yard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604226927p2/35482263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3158075789?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gumble&#39;s Yard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gumble&#39;s Yard" href="/user/show/35482263-gumble-s-yard">Gumble&#39;s Yard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780995632011" href="/book/show/52727606-el-llano-in-flames">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35482263-gumble-s-yard?shelf=2020-republic-of-consciousness-long">2020-republic-of-consciousness-long</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35482263-gumble-s-yard?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3158075789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9136899946789889015">I read this book due to its longlisting for the 2020 Republic of Consciousness Prize for UK small presses.<br/><br/>It is the first full length publication of Structo Press, which is associated with the literary magazine Structo which contains “remarkable new short stories and poetry from all around the world, alongside essays and interviews with authors and others ….on the fiction side we tend towards the slipstream end of things, and encourage submission of works in translation.”. Structo Press has prev</span>
  <span id="freeText9136899946789889015" style="display:none">I read this book due to its longlisting for the 2020 Republic of Consciousness Prize for UK small presses.<br /><br />It is the first full length publication of Structo Press, which is associated with the literary magazine Structo which contains “remarkable new short stories and poetry from all around the world, alongside essays and interviews with authors and others ….on the fiction side we tend towards the slipstream end of things, and encourage submission of works in translation.”. Structo Press has previously published chap books.<br /><br />The book is a translation of a classic (it may be more accurate to say the classic) Spanish language short-story collection: “El Llano en llamas” by Juan Rulfo; whose only other book was the novel “Pedro Páramo” widely regarded as the most famous 20th Century work in Spanish literature and as a precursor to Magic Realism. <br /><br />The collection (published in 1953) has had two well-known US English language translations but this translation by Stephen Beechinor represents the first UK translation. By contrast to the previous translation – Ilan Stevens in 2012 – this book has UK-English and also (perhaps in keeping with the much lower penetration of Spanish language in the UK) has retained very few Spanish terms other than proper names (the author regards El Llano as falling into that category). <br /><br />It certainly read very naturally to a UK-English reader – I cannot recall a single jarring note. I cannot judge the fidelity of the translation, but the translator said he wanted to capture Rulfo’s “stark, spare style” and this seems to work well. I would have preferred (as with all translated books) to have seen a more detailed translator’s note on some of the choices made (both around style and some individual phrases/words) to go with the excellent foreword by Dylan Brennan.<br /><br />The stories (17 of them over less than 180 pages) fall into a similar pattern – as one of the characters says <br /><br /><i>“You must think I’m doing here is rehashing the same idea over and again.”.</i><br /><br />They are set in a post-war (and more importantly post-Revolution) Mexican countryside, struggling with the failed impact of impractical and likely corrupt land reform whose main result appears to have been to have lead to mass migration from village to town and towns to cities while leaving things in the countryside somewhere between unchanged and even worse – a sense captured particularly strongly in the two opening stories “They gave us the land” (men trekking to their allotted but completely uncultivable land) and “La Cuesta de las Comrades” (a story of murder, justice and revenge). <br /><br />The countryside left behind is racked with poverty (“Because We’re So Poor” – about a girl likely to follow her sisters into prostitution after the loss of her cow) and prone to natural disasters (“The Day in Ruins” is the closest to a satirical story in the collection – of a voluble but insincere politician visiting a small town destroyed in an earthquake). <br /><br />But most of all this is a brutal land of violence and retribution which is matched in the terse and brutal nature of the language used – language which is often effectively told in the form of monologues – either internal or oral storytelling. <br /><br />Many of the stories feature bandits/revolutionaries (“The Man” moves between a fugitive murderer and a pursuer bent on revenge; “At First Light” a peasant on trial for murdering his patron; “El Llano in Flames” – a vicious and retributive series of massacres between Federals and rebels; “Tell them not to kill me” – a man is brought to justice for a murder he committed years ago (by the victim’s son – now an army officer) and pleads unsuccessfully with his indifferent/self-interested son to beg for his life; “The Night They Left Him Alone” – a fugitive and wanted murderer/rebel fleeing with his relatives, is unable to keep pace with his relatives – leading to them falling into an army ambush rather than him) <br /><br />Others more family or personal related violence (“Talpa” – a man and his sister-in-law take the man’s badly ill brother/girl’s husband on a pilgrimage that they know/hope will likely kill him; “You Don’t Hear The Dog Barking” – a man carries his badly injured son towards a town for assistance, all the time berating him for his pas life; “The legacy of Matilde Arcangel” – a wife’s death blamed on a crying baby causing a horse to bolt leads to life-long enmity between a father and son; “Anacleto Morales” – a group of widows seek to force their ex-lover to assist with the canonisation of someone he actually killed in a thieves falling out).<br /> <br />Two stories that particularly struck me:<br /><br />“Paso del Norte” – a kind of “American Dirt” of poverty written economic migration and border violence written some 65+ years previously and without a hint of cultural appropriation.<br /><br />“Luvina” – the only one of the stories I felt which departed from the stark style of the other books and I believe was a step towards the ghost-world and magic realism of the author’s famous novel. “Luvina” is an almost fabled deserted town whose harsh landscape and ghostly inhabitants are described by a drunk ex-schoolteacher to his potential successor – a man travelling to the town for the first time. <br /><br />It also has some of the most descriptive language – albeit not departing from the general theme of abandonment and despair.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>From whatever angle you look at it, Luvina is an awful sorry place. You being headed there, you’ll soon realise. I would say its where sorrow makes her nest. Never a smile to be seen, and everyone goes around with a face on them like it got set in a cast. And, should you want, you can see that sorrow any time you please. The wind blowing there gets it stirred up, yet never sweeps it away. It lingers there like it was born there. And you get to know it by taste and by feel, from the way it bears down on you constantly, clutching you tight, like a terrible poultice crushed into the heart’s vital flesh</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />A worthy addition to the longlist – if perhaps unusual as being by an author dead for more than 30 years.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9136899946789889015" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3158113486" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3158113486" />
    <a title="Hugh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32318315-hugh"><img alt="Hugh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466451007p2/32318315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3158113486?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hugh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hugh" href="/user/show/32318315-hugh">Hugh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780995632011" href="/book/show/52727606-el-llano-in-flames">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=translations">translations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=r-of-c-2020">r-of-c-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3158113486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3236463796163317813"><b>Longlisted for the Republic of Consciousness Prize 2020</b><br/><br/>I finished this a couple of days ago, and I am still not sure how I feel about it. Rulfo's bleak and often violent portraits of hard lives on the Mexican llano or plain, told in simple, stark, spare language are undoubtedly impressively realised, but it is difficult to assess this new translation of a book that is 70 years old without having read the earlier translations aimed more at an American audience, or his more famous novella <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38787.Pedro_P_ramo' title='Pedro Páramo by Juan Rulfo' rel='nofollow noopener'> Pedro P</a></span>
  <span id="freeText3236463796163317813" style="display:none"><b>Longlisted for the Republic of Consciousness Prize 2020</b><br /><br />I finished this a couple of days ago, and I am still not sure how I feel about it. Rulfo's bleak and often violent portraits of hard lives on the Mexican llano or plain, told in simple, stark, spare language are undoubtedly impressively realised, but it is difficult to assess this new translation of a book that is 70 years old without having read the earlier translations aimed more at an American audience, or his more famous novella <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38787.Pedro_P_ramo" title="Pedro Páramo by Juan Rulfo" rel="nofollow noopener"> Pedro Páramo</a>, let alone to compare it with another 11 recently written books. <br /><br />I can't say I particularly enjoyed reading it either, but I can see how Rulfo's style influenced later writers.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3236463796163317813" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3158107690" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3158107690" />
    <a title="Neil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33119635-neil"><img alt="Neil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544045847p2/33119635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3158107690?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil" href="/user/show/33119635-neil">Neil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780995632011" href="/book/show/52727606-el-llano-in-flames">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33119635-neil?shelf=2020-rofc">2020-rofc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33119635-neil?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3158107690" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7417545162621559686">This is a collection of short stories that deserves at least one re-read, probably more. It feels like the kind of book that would reward multiple readings.<br/><br/>I have to confess to a complete lack of knowledge of Rulfo until this book was long listed for the 2020 Republic of Consciousness Prize. Given that a quick search on the Internet reveals that it is one of the most important Spanish language collections of short stories (quite possibly the most important), that’s clearly my misfortune and some</span>
  <span id="freeText7417545162621559686" style="display:none">This is a collection of short stories that deserves at least one re-read, probably more. It feels like the kind of book that would reward multiple readings.<br /><br />I have to confess to a complete lack of knowledge of Rulfo until this book was long listed for the 2020 Republic of Consciousness Prize. Given that a quick search on the Internet reveals that it is one of the most important Spanish language collections of short stories (quite possibly the most important), that’s clearly my misfortune and something for which to be grateful to the prize.<br /><br />The stories are set in the Mexican countryside where people struggle to eke out a living on land that is unresponsive and where communities have been deserted in favour of towns and cities. In the first story, we read of men taking journey out to their allotted land which is almost completely barren - a storm threatens but just a single drop of rain falls. This sets the tone for the remaining 16 stories.<br /><br />My favourite story is the third one, titled, very simply “The man”. It is structured in a way that is similar to the book I read immediately prior to this one (Love by Hanne Orstavik) in that it runs two narratives in parallel switching between them without pause or any indication. In this story, a man and his pursuer narrate.<br /><br />Whilst “The man” was my favourite of the stories, all the stories are strong. Several of them are written as though spoken by the narrator to some nearby listeners. All of them are economical with words.<br /><br />At first sight, it seems unusual for the RoC Prize to include a collection of stories that was first published almost 70 years ago, but the book does not feel out of place alongside its 11 companions on the longlist (it’s the seventh one I’ve read).</span>
  <a data-text-id="7417545162621559686" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1337829053" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1337829053" />
    <a title="Steven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4452312-steven"><img alt="Steven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449093910p2/4452312.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1337829053?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven" href="/user/show/4452312-steven">Steven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780292743854" href="/book/show/13699329-the-plain-in-flames">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1337829053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18140398341025237412"><blockquote><i>We don't say what we are thinking. We lost the will to speak a while back. We lost it because of the heat. You would be happy to talk elsewhere, but it is hard here. You talk here and the words become hot in your mouth from the outside heat, and they dry on your tongue until the breath is gone.</i> (2)</blockquote>After having greatly appreciated Rulfo's novel <i>Pedro Páramo</i>, I decided to read his other major work, the short stories collected in <i>The Plain in Flames</i>, which took me surprisingly long to finish – all </span>
  <span id="freeText18140398341025237412" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>We don't say what we are thinking. We lost the will to speak a while back. We lost it because of the heat. You would be happy to talk elsewhere, but it is hard here. You talk here and the words become hot in your mouth from the outside heat, and they dry on your tongue until the breath is gone.</i> (2)</blockquote>After having greatly appreciated Rulfo's novel <i>Pedro Páramo</i>, I decided to read his other major work, the short stories collected in <i>The Plain in Flames</i>, which took me surprisingly long to finish – all in all I spent a week on its 122 pages. That was partly because I was also reading Edward Said and Walter Benjamin on the side, but mostly due to the nature of Rulfo's stories, which are very dense and emotionally taxing. You certainly feel, in each one of them, Rulfo's Mexico pressing and beating down upon you, with all its heat and dust. There was one story that I did not like, called <i>At Dawn</i>. The thing is, I have very high tolerance for all sorts of subjects in writing; you really cannot make a tale too depressing or bleak for me. But cruelty to animals is something that I despise and for which I have no tolerance or understanding whatsoever. I simply cannot do anything with a little calf being senselessly beaten in the face, as happens in <i>At Dawn</i> – other than, in fairness to Rulfo, to try not to let my disgust extend from the story to him as a writer or person. Aside from this story, and one other called <i>Talpa</i> that was also rather disgusting, yet only in a bodily sense, I thought that The Plain in Flames was fantastic. Several of the stories were truly exquisite, like <i>You Don't Hear Dogs Barking</i>, which became an instant favorite. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18140398341025237412" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2277014032" />
    <a title="John Farebrother" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother"><img alt="John Farebrother" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499497213p2/68609816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2277014032?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Farebrother" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Farebrother" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother">John Farebrother</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2277014032" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3527704822470012861">An awesome book. A series of related short stories from Mexico's turbulent revolutionary past. But the magic of the book is perhaps not the events of the stories themselves, as poignant and relevant as they are, ranging from childhood to nuns to livestock rustling. What I really enjoyed is the evocative language, the descriptions of people and places that make them come alive, and place the reader in that time and space. The gritty violence and desperate poverty that made up everyday life - in a</span>
  <span id="freeText3527704822470012861" style="display:none">An awesome book. A series of related short stories from Mexico's turbulent revolutionary past. But the magic of the book is perhaps not the events of the stories themselves, as poignant and relevant as they are, ranging from childhood to nuns to livestock rustling. What I really enjoyed is the evocative language, the descriptions of people and places that make them come alive, and place the reader in that time and space. The gritty violence and desperate poverty that made up everyday life - in a way that hasn't changed much for many people today, the internet and mobile phones notwithstanding. But perhaps above all the outdoor life, a familiarity with a sometimes cruel nature that is no longer part of modern life. A true classic of Central American literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3527704822470012861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_239485130" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/239485130" />
    <a title="Alejandro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4775751-alejandro"><img alt="Alejandro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321989967p2/4775751.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/239485130?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alejandro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alejandro" href="/user/show/4775751-alejandro">Alejandro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer239485130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9732591603256803560">It reads like a book of fables with no clear moral, except an uncompromising look at the solitude, uncertainty and the infinite weight of fate in early 20th century Mexico. It has many fun and lighthearted moments, mixed (as it happens in life) with really sad and frustrating ones. <br/>If you can read it in spanish, do. Half of it's value resides in the language the characters use. Simultaneously authentic and poetic. <br/><br/>Lo sentí como un libro de fábulas sin moraleja clara. Únicamente una mirada despia</span>
  <span id="freeText9732591603256803560" style="display:none">It reads like a book of fables with no clear moral, except an uncompromising look at the solitude, uncertainty and the infinite weight of fate in early 20th century Mexico. It has many fun and lighthearted moments, mixed (as it happens in life) with really sad and frustrating ones. <br />If you can read it in spanish, do. Half of it's value resides in the language the characters use. Simultaneously authentic and poetic. <br /><br />Lo sentí como un libro de fábulas sin moraleja clara. Únicamente una mirada despiadada a la soledad, incertidumbre y el peso infinito del destino en el temprano siglo XX en México. No le faltan momentos divertidos, situaciones ligeras y graciosas. Pero vienen mezcladas (como en la vida) con sucesos tristes y frustrantes. <br />La mitad de su valor reside en el lenguaje, tanto del narrador como en los diálogos entre personajes. Logra ser simultáneamente poético y atuéntico, natural. Tal vez el mismo Rulfo no creyó superarse a sí mismo en este sentido; tal vez por eso no lo intentó. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9732591603256803560" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8608726200311010721">A collection of searing tales from the wilds of early 20th century Mexico. I quite enjoyed it, I’ve got another one in my bag waiting to be read.</span>

<div id="review_840676522" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/840676522" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3143117-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571421692p2/3143117.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/840676522?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3143117-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789685208581" href="/book/show/214606.El_llano_en_llamas">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=espa%C3%B1ol">español</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=mexican-lit">mexican-lit</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer840676522" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1893576878240939744">I'll be honest. I started this book six months ago and found the first few stories didn't grab me. Then I picked it up this week and couldn't put it down. <br/><br/>The stories come in all sort of themes. There is the boy (Macario) who kept eating so he would stay alive and couldn't die because his godmother kept threatening that he will to purgatory instead of going to heaven to see his parents. A traveller stops in the bar in the driest place on earth and is offered the mescal that won't let him leave (</span>
  <span id="freeText1893576878240939744" style="display:none">I'll be honest. I started this book six months ago and found the first few stories didn't grab me. Then I picked it up this week and couldn't put it down. <br /><br />The stories come in all sort of themes. There is the boy (Macario) who kept eating so he would stay alive and couldn't die because his godmother kept threatening that he will to purgatory instead of going to heaven to see his parents. A traveller stops in the bar in the driest place on earth and is offered the mescal that won't let him leave (Luvina). Then there is the very surreal and amusing incident of a man, Lucas who visited by the group of women who want a local to be sainted although Lucas claims otherwise (Anecleto Morones) and the ending delivers a good laugh. The longest story and the one this collection is named for is a gritty story set during the Mexican Revolution and we find that even if your father was on one side, it meant you would be punished as well, despite whatever your views. <br /><br />Most of the stories are short and the full of twists. Rulfo's pace is wonderful, often filling the stories with atmosphere and mood, so one knows that something was about to happen but you just didn't know what. After reading this collection of stories, and of course his novel, "Pedro Paramo", one can see how this major Mexican writer influenced Gabriel Garcia Marquez. <br /><br />Lastly, I have a small translation quibble. The English book is known as "The Burning Plain" but it literally reads as "The Plain in Flames" which has a closer style of alliteration of the "ll", "e" and "a" of "El llano en llamas".<br /><br />Read in Spanish.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1893576878240939744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16042411453315920955"><i>the burning plain and other stories (el llano en llamas)</i> is one of the two works juan rulfo published in his lifetime (the short novel <i>pedro páramo</i> being the other). while hardly prolific, the mexican author is widely considered one of latin america's most influential writers. the fifteen stories contained in this collection are all rather harsh and merciless, much like the landscape rulfo knew so well. set around the time of the mexican revolution, these tales convey everyday rural hardships, m</span>
  <span id="freeText16042411453315920955" style="display:none"><i>the burning plain and other stories (el llano en llamas)</i> is one of the two works juan rulfo published in his lifetime (the short novel <i>pedro páramo</i> being the other). while hardly prolific, the mexican author is widely considered one of latin america's most influential writers. the fifteen stories contained in this collection are all rather harsh and merciless, much like the landscape rulfo knew so well. set around the time of the mexican revolution, these tales convey everyday rural hardships, many of which deal with poverty and death. written in a prosaic style and lacking the stylistic richness of the magical realism he is credited with inspiring, <i>the burning plain</i> is an unidealized portrait of life's adversities. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1697357371727178192">If Death were to keep a journal, it would probably be very much like Rulfo's <i>Burning Plain and Other Stories</i>. Within these pages, Death's exploits are detailed as he slowly, patiently pursues his victims, torturing them beyond recognition, reducing them to the mere desire to live. Sounds terrible, I know, but I couldn't put the book down. Rulfo's words though unadorned and straightforward possess an incredible power capable of drawing the reader into a grim, waterless world. There isn't even any</span>
  <span id="freeText1697357371727178192" style="display:none">If Death were to keep a journal, it would probably be very much like Rulfo's <i>Burning Plain and Other Stories</i>. Within these pages, Death's exploits are detailed as he slowly, patiently pursues his victims, torturing them beyond recognition, reducing them to the mere desire to live. Sounds terrible, I know, but I couldn't put the book down. Rulfo's words though unadorned and straightforward possess an incredible power capable of drawing the reader into a grim, waterless world. There isn't even any good food or drink to break up the horror of life. <i>The Burning Plain and Other Stories</i> is an amazing book easily worthy of 5 stars. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer122263043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2084745714311570843">Very exacting stories. Rulfo wrote only two books in his lifetime. The other is the acclaimed <i>Pedro Páramo</i>. This collection of short stories is an excursion into an inhospitable environment. Rulfo immerses you into the ugly and banal side of human nature. It's full of conflicts and peopled by criminals, adulterers, and rebels. The strange thing is that despite the ugliness described, the beauty of the writing comes across very well. It must be hard to balance this kind of thing.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3066282851065282581">Cryptic little stories of rural Mexican poverty, and very, very clearly an influence on Cormac McCarthy's Border Trilogy -- stories of desperation and primal joy, told amid a landscape of pinon trees and sandstone outcrops. I don't know enough about Mexican literary history to know where Rulfo fits in, but I know in a broader sense that the firm of Garcia Marquez & Sons, Magical Realists cited Rulfo as a major influence. And while Garcia Marquez writes jungles and Rulfo writes deserts (in both c</span>
  <span id="freeText3066282851065282581" style="display:none">Cryptic little stories of rural Mexican poverty, and very, very clearly an influence on Cormac McCarthy's Border Trilogy -- stories of desperation and primal joy, told amid a landscape of pinon trees and sandstone outcrops. I don't know enough about Mexican literary history to know where Rulfo fits in, but I know in a broader sense that the firm of Garcia Marquez &amp; Sons, Magical Realists cited Rulfo as a major influence. And while Garcia Marquez writes jungles and Rulfo writes deserts (in both cases, both literally and figuratively), the commonalities are easy enough to find. Both use tales of Latin campesinos to tell much bigger stories about our position in the universe, and even when Rulfo gets dark, there's a strongly human core to his work.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3230299458" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3230299458" />
    <a title="1000Nights&amp;AKnight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/86278215-1000nights-aknight"><img alt="1000Nights&amp;AKnight" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585311659p2/86278215.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3230299458?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="1000Nights&amp;AKnight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="1000Nights&amp;AKnight" href="/user/show/86278215-1000nights-aknight">1000Nights&amp;AKnight</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/86278215-1000nights-aknight?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3230299458" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9439435184903673091">Juan Rulfo is a master acknowledged by several luminaries of the Latin American boom, Marquez and Borges among them. This seems odd, given how different Rulfo's taciturn style is from the voluminous, romantic, 'magical' style of these luminaries, and he is probably best thought of as a 'writer's writer', one to be studied but not necessarily imitated. <br/><br/>There is certainly much to be admired in this collection, but rather less to be enjoyed. I would single out Rulfo's style for praise, especially i</span>
  <span id="freeText9439435184903673091" style="display:none">Juan Rulfo is a master acknowledged by several luminaries of the Latin American boom, Marquez and Borges among them. This seems odd, given how different Rulfo's taciturn style is from the voluminous, romantic, 'magical' style of these luminaries, and he is probably best thought of as a 'writer's writer', one to be studied but not necessarily imitated. <br /><br />There is certainly much to be admired in this collection, but rather less to be enjoyed. I would single out Rulfo's style for praise, especially its economy and discipline. There is no self-indulgence, no longuers, no author stand-ins, no philosophy. The pathos of each situation simply speaks for itself. The only instance of philosophy I recall is when the narrator of the story 'La herencia de Matilde Arcángel' says something slightly bathetic: 'Y ustedes y yo y todos sabemos que el tiempo es más pesado que la más pesada carga que puede soportar el hombre.' Rulfo pays great attention to species of plants, birds and other animals; one senses that his background is 'real', constantly in his mind's eye, and he is able to invoke the harsh Mexican landscape without laboured descriptions of it.<br /><br />Four stars for Rulfo's style then. But only two stars for his material. Almost all the stories deal with the rather miserable, hard-scrabble lives of peasants and outlaws living in the wake of the Mexican revolution. Almost all of them have tragic endings for the principals, usually death for the men and sexual disgrace for the women. These always strike me as the cheapest of endings for a short story, an unimaginative way of tying things up and giving the reader a 'punch'. Only the men can be said to have agency and independence while the women, invariably wives and daughters, usually end as victims. <br /><br />Rulfo was therefore not very imaginative in his themes and, despite its shortness, the collection (17 short stories, each 5 pages long, on average) was something of a slog. Each sitting with it felt like the prospect of another oppresive dinner of healthy but plain fare with a well-dressed, well-mannered but spiritually burdened and untalkative host in a tidy but small, dimly lit and sparsely furnished room with almost nothing to delight the eye, the palate or the mind. Four stories broke (or at least cracked) the general mould, when it was as if some beloved had walked into the room and Rulfo had decided to lighten up slightly, displaying a keen sensitiveness for the ironies of life: 'Nos han dado la tierra', 'La herencia de Matilde Arcángel', 'El día del derrumbe' and 'Anacleto Morones'. <br /><br />In 'Anacleto Morones' a group of old ladies wish to canonize the titular character and visit his son-in-law to recruit him for their pilgrimage to a priest. In the course of their interview the villanies of the prospective 'saint' (who has just escaped from prison), his daughter, the ladies and the son-in-law are all comically revealed before the story suddenly ends on a very dark, murderous note. In these and the three other stories named one senses what was missing from the rest of the collection: humour of any kind.<br /><br />Author Rulfo most me reminded me of: Graciliano Ramos.<br /><br />Collections of short stories which I think are better: '<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/244261.The_Street_of_Crocodiles" title="The Street of Crocodiles by Bruno Schulz" rel="nofollow noopener">The Street of Crocodiles</a>' and '<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/89064.The_Red_Commissar_Including_Further_Adventures_Of_The_Good_Soldier_Svejk_And_Other_Stories" title="The Red Commissar Including Further Adventures Of The Good Soldier Svejk And Other Stories by Jaroslav Hašek" rel="nofollow noopener">The Red Commissar: Including Further Adventures Of The Good Soldier Svejk And Other Stories</a>'.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9439435184903673091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_101359758" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101359758" />
    <a title="Mochizuki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2435459-mochizuki"><img alt="Mochizuki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1511144492p2/2435459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101359758?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mochizuki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mochizuki" href="/user/show/2435459-mochizuki">Mochizuki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2435459-mochizuki?shelf=hispano-american">hispano-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101359758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer931937071793834867">“The burning plain” is the longest story in the collection that bears its name. This is the first story that gives the reader insight into what the historical moment of the Revolution was like, and it does a particularly effective job of eroding away the mythical veneer that makes it seem to be a movement by and for the inspired, morally just masses. Narrated in first person, “El llano en llamas” makes the Revolution seem like little more than a celebration of “machista” brotherhood. Here Rulfo </span>
  <span id="freeText931937071793834867" style="display:none">“The burning plain” is the longest story in the collection that bears its name. This is the first story that gives the reader insight into what the historical moment of the Revolution was like, and it does a particularly effective job of eroding away the mythical veneer that makes it seem to be a movement by and for the inspired, morally just masses. Narrated in first person, “El llano en llamas” makes the Revolution seem like little more than a celebration of “machista” brotherhood. Here Rulfo adds little to the description of the nature of the male revolutionary already captured by other previous writers (men like Pedro are violent on and off the battlefield and ignorant of the far-reaching repercussions generated by their actions). Supposedly an emancipatory movement, the Revolution is ironically characterized by violence and betrayal so profound that — when the fighting is over — it is hard to imagine a way forward. <br /><br />In fact, one must ask if the “Revolution” ever really accomplished anything, or if it has even ended. This ambivalence is apparent in the final encounter of Pichón with the woman he raped and who bore his son. While he seems to recognize his own “mean look” in the boy’s face, the woman insists that he is no thief or murderer but rather a “good person.” Much like the promises of the post-revolutionary politicians, despite the mother’s assurances it is difficult to say with any certainty what the boy’s future holds. <br /><br />Perhaps “The burning plain” best captures the ambivalence of the Revolution in the character of Pedro Zamora- Pedro is much more than a small time bandit, he is a revolutionary caudillo. Latin American caudillos (charismatic populist leaders who combined political and military strength in order to act as strongmen or warlords) who were were common in the 19th and into the 20th century. As much as one rejects Pedro’s violent tactics, he is still considered a great leader by his men. His calm nature and particularly his watchful, piercing eyes are much admired by the narrator. In this manner his men feel protected around him, and protection is exactly what caudillos offered the campesinos who lived in the areas under their influence. The admiration expressed in “The burning plain” for the figure of the caudillo is rather remarkable, since normally realist narrative treated them as nothing more than oppressors. Rulfo effectively communicates the apparent need men have for a powerful father, while also recognizing the importance of the mediating ethical role played by the figure of the mother in the story. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="931937071793834867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
